<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle;"><img alt="" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="573" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Vergina_Sun_-_Golden_Larnax.png/20px-Vergina_Sun_-_Golden_Larnax.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Vergina_Sun_-_Golden_Larnax.png/30px-Vergina_Sun_-_Golden_Larnax.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Vergina_Sun_-_Golden_Larnax.png/40px-Vergina_Sun_-_Golden_Larnax.png 2x" width="20"/></td>

<tr>
<td colspan="3"><small><span id="inforefa"></span>a. <b><a href="#infoa">^</a></b> Native language.<br/>
<span id="inforefb"></span>b. <b><a href="#infob">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">Official language</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><br/>
<span id="inforefc"></span>c. <b><a href="#infoc">^</a></b> Literary language in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Babylonia" title="Achaemenid Babylonia">Babylonia</a>.</small></td>

</table>
<p>The <b>Achaemenid Empire</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/iː/ long 'e' in 'seed'">iː</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>, from <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> <i>Haxāmanišiya</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap">c. 550–330 BC</span>), also called the <b>(First) Persian Empire</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GCS_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-GCS-11">[11]</a></sup> was an <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">empire</a> based in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>. At its greatest extent from the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley" title="Indus Valley">Indus Valley</a>, it was one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires" title="List of largest empires">largest empires in history</a>, spanning 5.5 million square kilometers, and was larger than any previous empire in history. It is equally notable for its successful model of a centralised, bureaucratic administration (through <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satraps</a> under the <a href="/wiki/King_of_Kings" title="King of Kings">King of Kings</a>), for building infrastructure such as <a href="/wiki/Royal_Road" title="Royal Road">road systems</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Chapar_Khaneh" title="Chapar Khaneh">postal system</a> and the use of an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> across its territories and a large professional army and civil services, inspiring similar systems in later empires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmitt-EI-i_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-schmitt-EI-i-12">[12]</a></sup> It is noted in Western history as the antagonist of the <a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">Greek city-states</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a> and for the emancipation of the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">Jewish exiles in Babylon</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_at_Halicarnassus" title="Mausoleum at Halicarnassus">Mausoleum at Halicarnassus</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Ancient_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Ancient World">Seven Wonders of the Ancient World</a>, was built in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_architecture" title="Ancient Greek architecture">a Hellenistic style</a> in the empire as well.</p>
<p>By the 7th century BC, the <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> had settled in the southwestern portion of the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a> in the region of <a href="/wiki/Persis" title="Persis">Persis</a>, which came to be their heartland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-book-14">[14]</a></sup> From this region, <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> advanced to defeat the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a>, establishing the Achaemenid Empire. The delegation of power to local governments is thought to have eventually weakened the king's authority, causing resources to be expended in attempts to subdue local rebellions, and leading to the disunity of the region at the time of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">invasion</a> in 334 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-book-14">[14]</a></sup> This viewpoint, however, is challenged by some modern scholars who argue that the Achaemenid Empire was not facing any such crisis around the time of Alexander, and that only internal succession struggles within the Achaemenid family ever came close to weakening the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-book-14">[14]</a></sup> Alexander, an avid admirer of Cyrus the Great,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulrich_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ulrich-15">[15]</a></sup> would eventually conquer the empire in its entirety by 330 BC. Upon his death most of the empire's former territory came under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, in addition to other minor territories which gained independence at that time. The Persian population of the central plateau would eventually reclaim power by the second century BC under the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-book-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The historical mark of the Achaemenid Empire went far beyond its territorial and military influences and included cultural, social, technological and religious influences as well. Many <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athenians</a> adopted Achaemenid customs in their daily lives in a reciprocal cultural exchange,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> some being employed by, or allied to the Persian kings. The impact of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus%27s_edict" title="Cyrus's edict">Cyrus's edict</a> is mentioned in Judeo-Christian texts and the empire was instrumental in the spread of <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> as far east as <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. The Empire would also set the tone for the politics, heritage and history of modern Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Achaemenes" title="Achaemenes">Achaemenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teispids" title="Teispids">Teispids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_family_tree" title="Achaemenid family tree">Achaemenid family tree</a></div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Persian nation contains a number of tribes as listed here. ... : the Pasargadae, Maraphii, and Maspii, upon which all the other tribes are dependent. Of these, the Pasargadae are the most distinguished; they contain the clan of the Achaemenids from which spring the Perseid kings. Other tribes are the Panthialaei, Derusiaei, Germanii, all of which are attached to the soil, the remainder -the Dai, Mardi, Dropici, Sagarti, being nomadic.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a> 1.101 &amp; 125</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg/220px-The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg/330px-The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg/440px-The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_maussolleion_model_dsc02711-miniaturk_nevit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_at_Halicarnassus" title="Mausoleum at Halicarnassus">Mausoleum at Halicarnassus</a>, one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_wonders_of_the_ancient_world" title="Seven wonders of the ancient world">Seven wonders of the ancient world</a>, was built by Greek architects for the local Persian <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> of <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mausolus" title="Mausolus">Mausolus</a> (Scale model)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Achaemenid Empire was created by nomadic <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>. The name "Persia" is a Greek and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> pronunciation of the native word referring to people originating from Persis (or in Persian, <a href="/wiki/Fars_Province" title="Fars Province">Pars</a>), their home territory located north of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-enc-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite its success and rapid expansion, the Achaemenid Empire was not the first Iranian empire, as by 6th century BC another group of ancient <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian peoples</a> had already established the Median Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-enc-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> had originally been the dominant Iranian group in the region, rising to power at the end of the seventh century BC and incorporating the Persians into their empire. The Iranian people had arrived in the region c. 1000 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and had initially fallen under the domination of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> (911–609 BC). However, the Medes and Persians (together with the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a>) played a major role in the defeat of the Assyrians and establishment of the first Persian empire.</p>
<p>The term Achaemenid is in fact the <a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">Latinized</a> version of the Old Persian name <i>Haxāmaniš</i> (a <a href="/wiki/Bahuvrihi" title="Bahuvrihi">bahuvrihi</a> compound translating to "having a friend's mind"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schlerath_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-schlerath-20">[20]</a></sup>), meaning in Greek "of the family of the Achaemenis." Despite the derivation of the name, <a href="/wiki/Achaemenes" title="Achaemenes">Achaemenes</a> was himself a minor seventh-century ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Anshan_(Persia)" title="Anshan (Persia)">Anshan</a> (<i>Ansham</i> or <i>Anšān</i>) in southwestern Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-enc-18">[18]</a></sup> It was not until the time of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> (Cyrus II of Persia), a descendant of Achaemenes, that the Achaemenid Empire developed the prestige of an empire and set out to incorporate the existing empires of the ancient east, becoming the vast Persian Empire of ancient legend.</p>
<p>At some point in 550 BC, Cyrus rose in rebellion against the Medes (most likely due to their mismanagement of Persis), eventually conquering the Medes and creating the first Persian empire. Cyrus the Great utilized his tactical genius,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> as well as his understanding of the socio-political conditions governing his territories, to eventually incorporate into the Empire neighbouring Lydia and the Neo-Babylonian Empire, also leading the way for his successor, <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a>, to venture into Egypt and defeat the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-sixth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-sixth Dynasty of Egypt">Twenty-sixth Dynasty of Egypt</a>.</p>
<p>Cyrus the Great's political acumen was reflected in his management of his newly formed empire, as the Persian Empire became the first to attempt to govern many different ethnic groups on the principle of equal responsibilities and rights for all people, so long as subjects paid their taxes and kept the peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyv_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-cyv-22">[22]</a></sup> Additionally, the king agreed not to interfere with the local customs, religions, and trades of its subject states,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyv_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-cyv-22">[22]</a></sup> a unique quality that eventually won Cyrus the support of the Babylonians. This system of management ultimately became an issue for the Persians, as with a larger empire came the need for order and control, leading to expenditure of resources and mobilization of troops to quell local rebellions, and weakening the central power of the king. By the time of <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a>, this disorganization had almost led to a disunited realm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-book-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Persians from whom Cyrus hailed were originally <a href="/wiki/Nomadic_pastoralism" title="Nomadic pastoralism">nomadic pastoralists</a> in the western Iranian Plateau and by 850 BC were calling themselves the <i>Parsa</i> and their constantly shifting territory <i>Parsua</i>, for the most part localized around Persis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-book-14">[14]</a></sup> As Persians gained power, they developed the infrastructure to support their growing influence, including creation of a capital named <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> and an opulent city named <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>.</p>
<p>Begun during the rule of <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> "the Great" and completed some 100 years later,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gov_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-gov-23">[23]</a></sup> Persepolis was a symbol of the empire serving both as a ceremonial centre and a center of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gov_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-gov-23">[23]</a></sup> It had a special set of gradually progressive stairways named "All Countries"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gov_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-gov-23">[23]</a></sup> around which carved relief decoration depicted scenes of heroism, hunting, natural themes, and presentation of the gifts to the Achaemenid kings by their subjects during the spring festival, <a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Nowruz</a>. The core structure was composed of a multitude of square rooms or halls, the biggest of which was called <i><a href="/wiki/Apadana" title="Apadana">Apadana</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gov_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-gov-23">[23]</a></sup> Tall, decorated columns welcomed visitors and emphasized the height of the structure. Later on, Darius also utilized <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a> as his governmental centres, developing them to a similar metropolitan status.</p>
<p>Accounts of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_family_tree" title="Achaemenid family tree">Achaemenid family tree</a> can be derived from either documented Greek or Roman accounts, or from existing documented Persian accounts such as those found in the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>. However, since most existing accounts of this vast empire are in works of Greek philosophers and historians, and since many of the original Persian documents are lost, not to mention being subject to varying scholarly views on their origin and possible motivations behind them, it is difficult to create a definitive and completely objective list. Nonetheless, it is clear that Cyrus and Darius were critical in the expansion of the empire. Cyrus is often believed to be the son of <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_I" title="Cambyses I">Cambyses I</a>, grandson of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_I" title="Cyrus I">Cyrus I</a>, the father of <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a>, and a relative of Darius through a shared ancestor, <a href="/wiki/Teispes" title="Teispes">Teispes</a>. Cyrus the Great is also believed to have been a family member (possibly grandson) of the Median king <a href="/wiki/Astyages" title="Astyages">Astyages</a> through his mother, <a href="/wiki/Mandane_of_Media" title="Mandane of Media">Mandane of Media</a>. A minority of scholars argue that perhaps Achaemenes was a retrograde creation of Darius in order to reconcile his connection with Cyrus after gaining power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-enc-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greek</a> writers provide some legendary information about <a href="/wiki/Achaemenes" title="Achaemenes">Achaemenes</a> by calling his tribe the <i>Pasargadae</i> and stating that he was "raised by an <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagle</a>". <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, when writing about the Persians, identified Achaemenes with <a href="/wiki/Perses_(son_of_Andromeda_and_Perseus)" title="Perses (son of Andromeda and Perseus)">Perses</a>, ancestor of the Persians in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> According to Plato, Achaemenes was the same person as Perses, a son of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethiopian" title="Ethiopian">Ethiopian</a> queen <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_(mythology)" title="Andromeda (mythology)">Andromeda</a> and the Greek hero <a href="/wiki/Perseus" title="Perseus">Perseus</a>, and a grandson of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>. Later writers believed that Achaemenes and Perseus were different people, and that Perses was an ancestor of the king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dandamayev_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-dandamayev-25">[25]</a></sup> This account further confirms that Achaemenes could well have been a significant Anshan leader and an ancestor of Cyrus the Great. Regardless, both Cyrus the Great and Darius the Great were related, prominent kings of Persia, under whose rule the empire expanded to include much of the ancient world.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_expansion">Formation and expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Formation and expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_(flat_map).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="672" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg/350px-Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg/525px-Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg/700px-Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
