</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Persian_Border" title="Battle of the Persian Border">Battle of the Persian Border</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_Revolt" title="Persian Revolt">Persian Revolt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pteria" title="Battle of Pteria">Battle of Pteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Opis" title="Battle of Opis">Battle of Opis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pelusium_(525_BC)" title="Battle of Pelusium (525 BC)">Battle of Pelusium (525 BC)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_invasion_of_the_Indus_Valley" title="Achaemenid invasion of the Indus Valley">Achaemenid invasion of the Indus Valley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/European_Scythian_campaign_of_Darius_I" title="European Scythian campaign of Darius I">European Scythian campaign of Darius I</a></div>
<p>The empire took its unified form with a central administration around <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> erected by <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>. The empire ended up conquering and enlarging the Median Empire to include in addition many more territories, for example in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>. During the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> it engaged in military conflict with some of the major city-states of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, and although it came close to defeating the Greek army, this war ultimately led to the empire's overthrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 559 BC, <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_I" title="Cambyses I">Cambyses I</a> the Elder was succeeded as the king of Anšān by his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrus_The_Great" title="Cyrus The Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, who also succeeded the still-living <a href="/wiki/Arsames" title="Arsames">Arsames</a> as the King of Persia, thus reuniting the two realms. Cyrus is considered to be the first true king of the Persian Empire, as his predecessors were subservient to the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>. Cyrus the Great conquered Media, Lydia, and <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>. Cyrus was politically shrewd, modeling himself as the "savior" of conquered nations, often allowing displaced people to return, and giving his subjects freedom to practice local customs. To reinforce this image, he instituted policies of religious freedom, and restored temples and other infrastructure in the newly acquired cities (Most notably the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> inhabitants of Babylon, as recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_Cylinder" title="Cyrus Cylinder">Cyrus Cylinder</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>). As a result of his tolerant policies he came to be known by those of the Jewish faith as "the anointed of the Lord."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>His immediate successors were less successful. Cyrus' son <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a> conquered Egypt in 525 BC, but died in July 522 BC during a revolt led by a sacerdotal clan that had lost its power following Cyrus' conquest of Media. The cause of his death remains uncertain, although it may have been the result of an accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-camb_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-camb-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, Cambyses II had originally ventured into Egypt to take revenge for the pharaoh Amasis's trickery when he sent a fake Egyptian bride whose family Amasis had murdered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> instead of his own daughter, to wed Cambyses II. Additionally negative reports of mistreatment caused by Amasis, given by <a href="/wiki/Phanes_of_Halicarnassus" title="Phanes of Halicarnassus">Phanes of Halicarnassus</a>, a wise counsellor serving Amasis, further bolstered Cambyses's resolve to venture into Egypt. Amasis died before Cambyses II could face him, but his successor <a href="/wiki/Psamtik_III" title="Psamtik III">Psamtik III</a> was defeated by Cambyses II in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pelusium_(525_BC)" title="Battle of Pelusium (525 BC)">Battle of Pelusium</a>.</p>
<p>While Cambyses II was in Egypt, the Zoroastrian priests, whom Herodotus called <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">Magi</a>, usurped the throne for one of their own, <a href="/wiki/Gaumata" title="Gaumata">Gaumata</a>, who then pretended to be Cambyses II's younger brother <a href="/wiki/Bardiya" title="Bardiya">Bardiya</a> (Greek: <i>Smerdis</i> or <i>Tanaoxares/Tanyoxarkes</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-camb_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-camb-29">[29]</a></sup>), who had been assassinated some three years earlier. Owing to the strict rule of Cambyses II, especially his stance on taxation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and his long absence in Egypt, "the whole people, Perses, Medes and all the other nations," acknowledged the usurper, especially as he granted a remission of taxes for three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Cambyses II himself would not be able to quell the imposters, as he died on the way back from Egypt.</p>
<p>The claim that <a href="/wiki/Gaumata" title="Gaumata">Gaumata</a> had impersonated Bardiya (Smerdis), is derived from Darius the Great and the records at the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>. Historians are divided over the possibility that the story of the impostor was invented by Darius as justification for his coup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Darius made a similar claim when he later captured Babylon, announcing that the Babylonian king was not, in fact, Nebuchadnezzar III, but an impostor named Nidintu-bel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a>, Gaumata ruled for seven months before being overthrown in 522 BC by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> (Darius I) (Old Persian <i>Dāryavuš</i>, "who holds firm the good", also known as <i>Darayarahush</i> or Darius the Great). The Magi, though persecuted, continued to exist, and a year following the death of the first pseudo-Smerdis (Gaumata), saw a second pseudo-Smerdis (named Vahyazdāta) attempt a coup. The coup, though initially successful, failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> writes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> that the native leadership debated the best form of government for the empire. It was agreed that an <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a> would divide them against one another, and democracy would bring about <a href="/wiki/Ochlocracy" title="Ochlocracy">mob rule</a> resulting in a charismatic leader resuming the monarchy. Therefore, they decided a new monarch was in order, particularly since <i>they</i> were in a position to choose him. Darius I was chosen monarch from among the leaders. He was cousin to Cambyses II and Bardiya (Smerdis), claiming <a href="/wiki/Ariaramnes" title="Ariaramnes">Ariaramnes</a> as his ancestor.</p>
<p>The Achaemenids thereafter consolidated areas firmly under their control. It was <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> who, by sound and farsighted administrative planning, brilliant military maneuvering, and a humanistic world view, established the greatness of the Achaemenids and, in less than thirty years, raised them from an obscure tribe to a world power. It was during the reign of Darius the Great (Darius I) that <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> was built (518–516 BC) and which would serve as capital for several generations of Achaemenid kings. <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a> (<i>Hagmatāna</i> "City of Gatherings", modern: <a href="/wiki/Hamadan" title="Hamadan">Hamadan</a>) in Media was greatly expanded during this period and served as the summer capital.</p>
<p>Ever since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonian</a> king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amyntas_I" title="Amyntas I">Amyntas I</a> surrendered his country to the Persians in about 512-511, Macedonians and Persians were strangers no more as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-37">[37]</a></sup> Subjugation of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a> was part of Persian military operations initiated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> (521–486) in 513 - after immense preparations - a huge Achaemenid army invaded the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Scythian_campaign_of_Darius_I" title="European Scythian campaign of Darius I">tried to defeat</a> the European <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> roaming to the north of the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-37">[37]</a></sup> Darius' army subjugated several <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracian peoples</a>, and virtually all other regions that touch the European part of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, such as parts of nowadays <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, before it returned to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Darius left in Europe one of his commanders named <a href="/wiki/Megabazus" title="Megabazus">Megabazus</a> whose task was to accomplish conquests in the Balkans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-37">[37]</a></sup> The Persian troops subjugated gold-rich <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, the coastal Greek cities, as well as defeating and conquering the powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paeonians" title="Paeonians">Paeonians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoweReames2008239_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoweReames2008239-40">[40]</a></sup> Finally, Megabazus sent envoys to Amyntas, demanding acceptance of Persian domination, which the Macedonians did. The Balkans provided many soldiers for the multi-ethnic Achaemenid army. Many of the Macedonian and Persian elite intermarried, such as the Persian official <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bubares&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bubares (page does not exist)">Bubares</a> who married Amyntas' daughter, Gygaea. Family ties the Macedonian rulers Amyntas and Alexander enjoyed with Bubares ensured them good relations with the Persian kings Darius and <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_37-5"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-37">[37]</a></sup> The Persian invasion led indirectly to Macedonia's rise in power and Persia had some common interests in the Balkans; with Persian aid, the Macedonians stood to gain much at the expense of some Balkan tribes such as the Paeonians and Greeks. All in all, the Macedonians were "willing and useful Persian allies. Macedonian soldiers fought against Athens and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> in Xerxes' army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_37-6"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-37">[37]</a></sup> The Persians referred to both Greeks and Macedonians as <i><a href="/wiki/Yona" title="Yona">Yauna</a></i> ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionian_Greeks" title="Ionian Greeks">Ionians</a>", their term for "Greeks"), and to Macedonians specifically as <i>Yaunã Takabara</i> or "Greeks with hats that look like shields", possibly referring to the Macedonian <a href="/wiki/Kausia" title="Kausia">kausia</a> hat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iranian_queen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="450" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Iranian_queen.jpg/170px-Iranian_queen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Iranian_queen.jpg/255px-Iranian_queen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Iranian_queen.jpg/340px-Iranian_queen.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iranian_queen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Persian queen <a href="/wiki/Atossa" title="Atossa">Atossa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>'s wife and mother of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the 5th century BC the Kings of Persia were either ruling over or had subordinated territories encompassing not just all of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Plateau" title="Persian Plateau">Persian Plateau</a> and all of the territories formerly held by the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrian Empire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Egypt" title="Persian Egypt">Egypt</a>), but beyond this all of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Caucasus" title="Southern Caucasus">Southern Caucasus</a> and parts of the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, all of <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paeonia_(kingdom)" title="Paeonia (kingdom)">Paeonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a> to the north and west, most of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> coastal regions, parts of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> as far as the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Oxus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Jaxartes</a> to the north and north-east, the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> and the western <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus basin</a> (corresponding to modern Afghanistan and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>) to the far east, parts of northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> to the south, and parts of northern <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libya</a> to the south-west, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>, China, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UAE" title="UAE">UAE</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius.org_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius.org-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-behi_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-behi-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c_ramirez-faria_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-c_ramirez-faria-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greco-Persian_Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Greco-Persian Wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(August 2013)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek-Persian_duel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="859" data-file-width="868" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Greek-Persian_duel.jpg/220px-Greek-Persian_duel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Greek-Persian_duel.jpg/330px-Greek-Persian_duel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Greek-Persian_duel.jpg/440px-Greek-Persian_duel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek-Persian_duel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Greek <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplite</a> and Persian warrior depicted fighting, on an ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kylix_(drinking_cup)" title="Kylix (drinking cup)">kylix</a>, 5th century BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a> in 499 BC, and associated revolts in Aeolis, Doris, Cyprus and Caria, were military rebellions by several regions of Asia Minor against Persian rule, lasting from 499 to 493 BC. At the heart of the rebellion was the dissatisfaction of the Greek cities of Asia Minor with the tyrants appointed by Persia to rule them, along with the individual actions of two Milesian tyrants, <a href="/wiki/Histiaeus" title="Histiaeus">Histiaeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristagoras" title="Aristagoras">Aristagoras</a>. In 499 BC, the then tyrant of <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a>, Aristagoras, launched a joint expedition with the Persian satrap Artaphernes to conquer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naxos_(island)" title="Naxos (island)">Naxos</a>, in an attempt to bolster his position in Miletus (both financially and in terms of prestige). The mission was a debacle, and sensing his imminent removal as tyrant, Aristagoras chose to incite the whole of Ionia into rebellion against the Persian king Darius the Great.</p>
<p>The Persians continued to reduce the cities along the west coast that still held out against them, before finally imposing a peace settlement in 493 BC on Ionia that was generally considered to be both just and fair. The <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a> constituted the first major conflict between Greece and the Achaemenid Empire, and as such represents the first phase of the Greco-Persian Wars. Asia Minor had been brought back into the Persian fold, but Darius had vowed to punish Athens and Eretria for their support of the revolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Moreover, seeing that the political situation in Greece posed a continued threat to the stability of his Empire, he decided to embark on the conquest of all of Greece. The first campaign of the invasion was to bring the territories in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan" title="Balkan">Balkan</a> peninsula back within the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_companion_to_Ancient_Macedonia_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_companion_to_Ancient_Macedonia-49">[49]</a></sup> The Persian grip over these territories had loosened following the Ionian Revolt. In 492 BC, the Persian general <a href="/wiki/Mardonius" title="Mardonius">Mardonius</a> re-subjugated <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> and made <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedon</a> a fully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subordinate" title="Subordinate">subordinate</a> part of the empire; it had been a vassal as early as the late 6th century BC, but retained a great deal of autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_companion_to_Ancient_Macedonia_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-A_companion_to_Ancient_Macedonia-49">[49]</a></sup> However, in 490 BC the Persian forces were defeated by the Athenians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Battle of Marathon</a> and Darius would die before having the chance to launch an invasion of Greece.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="793" data-file-width="992" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/300px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/450px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/600px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing events of the first phases of the Greco-Persian Wars</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerxes_I_of_Persia" title="Xerxes I of Persia">Xerxes I</a> (485–465 BC, Old Persian <i>Xšayārša</i> "Hero Among Kings"), son of <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a>, vowed to complete the job. He organized a massive invasion aiming to conquer <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. His army entered Greece from the north, meeting little or no resistance through <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Greece)" title="Macedonia (Greece)">Macedonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>, but was delayed by a small Greek force for three days at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">Thermopylae</a>. A simultaneous naval battle at Artemisium was tactically indecisive as large storms destroyed ships from both sides. The battle was stopped prematurely when the Greeks received news of the defeat at Thermopylae and retreated. The battle was a strategic victory for the Persians, giving them uncontested control of Artemisium and the Aegean Sea.</p>
<p>Following his victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">Battle of Thermopylae</a>, Xerxes sacked the evacuated city of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and prepared to meet the Greeks at the strategic <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">Isthmus of Corinth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saronic_Gulf" title="Saronic Gulf">Saronic Gulf</a>. In 480 BC the Greeks won a decisive victory over the Persian fleet at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a> and forced Xerxes to retire to <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>. The land army which he left in Greece under <a href="/wiki/Mardonius" title="Mardonius">Mardonius</a> retook Athens but was eventually destroyed in 479 BC at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">Battle of Plataea</a>. The final defeat of the Persians at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mycale" title="Battle of Mycale">Mycale</a> encouraged the Greek cities of Asia to revolt, and the Persians lost all of their territories in Europe; Macedonia once again became independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_37-7"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_phase">Cultural phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cultural phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="507" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg/200px-Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg/300px-Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg/400px-Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyrus_II_le_Grand_et_les_H%C3%A9breux.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cyrus the Great king of Iran (ancient Persia) is said in the Bible to have liberated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">captives in Babylon</a> to resettle and rebuild <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, earning him an honored place in Judaism.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> was assassinated, he was succeeded by his eldest son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_I" title="Artaxerxes I">Artaxerxes I</a>. It was during his reign that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamite" title="Elamite">Elamite</a> ceased to be the language of government, and Aramaic gained in importance. It was probably during this reign that the solar calendar was introduced as the national calendar. Under Artaxerxes I, <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> became the <i>de facto</i> religion of state, and for this Artaxerxes I is today also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a> of that faith.</p>
<p>After Persia had been defeated at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Eurymedon" title="Battle of Eurymedon">Battle of Eurymedon</a> (469 BC), military action between Greece and Persia was halted. When Artaxerxes I took power, he introduced a new Persian strategy of weakening the Athenians by funding their enemies in Greece. This indirectly caused the Athenians to move the treasury of the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a> from the island of <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">Delos</a> to the Athenian acropolis. This funding practice inevitably prompted renewed fighting in 450 BC, where the Greeks attacked at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis_in_Cyprus_(450_BC)" title="Battle of Salamis in Cyprus (450 BC)">Battle of Cyprus</a>. After <a href="/wiki/Cimon" title="Cimon">Cimon</a>'s failure to attain much in this expedition, the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Callias" title="Peace of Callias">Peace of Callias</a> was agreed between <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argos" title="Argos">Argos</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> in 449 BC.</p>
<p>Artaxerxes I offered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_asylum" title="Political asylum">asylum</a> to <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">Themistocles</a>, who was the winner of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a>, after Themistocles was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ostracized" title="Ostracized">ostracized</a> from <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>. Also, Artaxerxes I gave him <a href="/wiki/Magnesia_on_the_Maeander" title="Magnesia on the Maeander">Magnesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myus" title="Myus">Myus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lampsacus" title="Lampsacus">Lampsacus</a> to maintain him in bread, meat, and wine. In addition, Artaxerxes I gave him <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaescepsis" title="Palaescepsis">Palaescepsis</a> to provide him with clothes, and he also gave him <a href="/wiki/Percote" title="Percote">Percote</a> with bedding for his house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Artaxerxes died in 424 BC at <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>, his body was taken to the tomb already built for him in the <a href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Rustam" title="Naqsh-e Rustam">Naqsh-e Rustam</a> Necropolis. It was Persian tradition that kings begin constructing their own tombs while they were still alive. Artaxerxes I was immediately succeeded by his eldest son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerxes_II" title="Xerxes II">Xerxes II</a>, who was the only legitimate son of Artaxerxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuhrt2013880_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuhrt2013880-51">[51]</a></sup> However, after a few days on the throne, he was assassinated while drunk by Pharnacyas and Menostanes on the orders of his illegitimate brother: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdianus" title="Sogdianus">Sogdianus</a> who apparently had gained the support of his regions. He reigned for six months and fifteen days before being captured by his half-brother, <a href="/wiki/Darius_II" title="Darius II">Ochus</a>, who had rebelled against him. Sogdianus was executed by being <a href="/wiki/Suffocation_in_ash" title="Suffocation in ash">suffocated in ash</a> because Ochus had promised he would not die by the sword, by poison or by hunger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Ochus then took the royal name Darius II. Darius' ability to defend his position on the throne ended the short power vacuum.</p>
<p>From 412 BC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_II_of_Persia" title="Darius II of Persia">Darius II</a>, at the insistence of <a href="/wiki/Tissaphernes" title="Tissaphernes">Tissaphernes</a>, gave support first to Athens, then to Sparta, but in 407 BC, Darius' son <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Younger" title="Cyrus the Younger">Cyrus the Younger</a> was appointed to replace Tissaphernes and aid was given entirely to Sparta which finally defeated Athens in 404 BC. In the same year, Darius fell ill and died in Babylon. His death gave an Egyptian rebel named <a href="/wiki/Amyrtaeus" title="Amyrtaeus">Amyrtaeus</a> the opportunity to throw off <a href="/wiki/Twenty-seventh_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-seventh Dynasty of Egypt">Persian control over Egypt</a>. At his death bed, Darius' Babylonian wife <a href="/wiki/Parysatis" title="Parysatis">Parysatis</a> pleaded with him to have her second eldest son Cyrus (the Younger) crowned, but Darius refused. Queen Parysatis favoured Cyrus more than her eldest son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a>. <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> relates (probably on the authority of <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">Ctesias</a>) that the displaced Tissaphernes came to the new king on his coronation day to warn him that his younger brother Cyrus (the Younger) was preparing to assassinate him during the ceremony. Artaxerxes had Cyrus arrested and would have had him executed if their mother Parysatis had not intervened. Cyrus was then sent back as Satrap of Lydia, where he prepared an armed rebellion. Cyrus hired an army of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ten_Thousand_Greek_mercenaries" title="Ten Thousand Greek mercenaries">Ten Thousand Greek mercenaries</a> and made his way deeper into Persia. The army of Cyrus was stopped by the royal Persian army of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cunaxa" title="Battle of Cunaxa">Cunaxa</a> in 401 BC, where Cyrus was killed. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ten_Thousand_Greek_Mercenaries" title="Ten Thousand Greek Mercenaries">Ten Thousand Greek Mercenaries</a> including <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> were now deep in Persian territory and were at risk of attack. So they searched for others to offer their services to but eventually had to return to Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> was the longest reigning of the Achaemenid kings and it was during this 45-year period of relative peace and stability that many of the monuments of the era were constructed. Artaxerxes moved the capital back to <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, which he greatly extended. Also the summer capital at <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a> was lavishly extended with gilded columns and roof tiles of silver and copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The extraordinary innovation of the Zoroastrian shrines can also be dated to his reign, and it was probably during this period that Zoroastrianism spread from <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> throughout <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. The construction of temples, though serving a religious purpose, was not a purely selfless act, as they also served as an important source of income. From the Babylonian kings, the Achaemenids had taken over the concept of a mandatory temple tax, a one-tenth tithe which all inhabitants paid to the temple nearest to their land or other source of income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> A share of this income called the <i>Quppu Sha Sharri</i>, "kings chest"—an ingenious institution originally introduced by <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">Nabonidus</a>—was then turned over to the ruler. In retrospect, Artaxerxes is generally regarded as an amiable man who lacked the moral fibre to be a really successful ruler. However, six centuries later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ardeshir_I" title="Ardeshir I">Ardeshir I</a>, founder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Persian_Empire" title="Second Persian Empire">second Persian Empire</a>, would consider himself Artaxerxes' successor, a grand testimony to the importance of Artaxerxes to the Persian psyche.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="700" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg/240px-Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg/360px-Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg/480px-Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Double_daric_330-300_obverse_CdM_Paris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daric" title="Daric">Daric</a> of Artaxerxes II</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Artaxerxes II became involved in a war with Persia's erstwhile allies, the <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Spartans</a>, who, under <a href="/wiki/Agesilaus_II" title="Agesilaus II">Agesilaus II</a>, invaded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. In order to redirect the Spartans' attention to Greek affairs, Artaxerxes II subsidized their enemies: in particular the <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athenians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinthians</a>. These subsidies helped to engage the <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Spartans</a> in what would become known as the <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_War" title="Corinthian War">Corinthian War</a>. In 386 BC, Artaxerxes II betrayed his allies and came to an arrangement with Sparta, and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Antalcidas" title="Treaty of Antalcidas">Treaty of Antalcidas</a> he forced his erstwhile allies to come to terms. This treaty restored control of the Greek cities of <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aeolis" title="Aeolis">Aeolis</a> on the Anatolian coast to the Persians, while giving Sparta dominance on the Greek mainland. In 385 BC he <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes%27_II_Cadusian_Campaign" title="Artaxerxes' II Cadusian Campaign">campaigned against the Cadusians</a>. Although successful against the Greeks, Artaxerxes II had more trouble with the <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egyptians</a>, who had successfully revolted against him at the beginning of his reign. An attempt to reconquer Egypt in 373 BC was completely unsuccessful, but in his waning years the Persians did manage to defeat a joint Egyptian–Spartan effort to conquer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Phoenicia" title="Achaemenid Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>. He quashed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Satraps" title="Revolt of the Satraps">Revolt of the Satraps</a> in 372–362 BC. He is reported to have had a number of wives. His main wife was <a href="/wiki/Stateira_(wife_of_Artaxerxes_II)" title="Stateira (wife of Artaxerxes II)">Stateira</a>, until she was poisoned by Artaxerxes II's mother Parysatis in about 400 BC. Another chief wife was a Greek woman of <a href="/wiki/Phocaea" title="Phocaea">Phocaea</a> named Aspasia (not the same as the concubine of <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">Pericles</a>). Artaxerxes II is said to have more than 115 sons from 350 wives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 358 BC Artaxerxes II died and was succeeded by his son <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a>. In 355 BC, Artaxerxes III forced <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> to conclude a peace which required the city's forces to leave <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and to acknowledge the independence of its rebellious allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Artaxerxes started a campaign against the rebellious <a href="/wiki/Cadusii" title="Cadusii">Cadusians</a>, but he managed to appease both of the Cadusian kings. One individual who successfully emerged from this campaign was Darius Codomannus, who later occupied the Persian throne as <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a>.</p>
<p>Artaxerxes III then ordered the disbanding of all the satrapal armies of Asia Minor, as he felt that they could no longer guarantee peace in the west and was concerned that these armies equipped the western satraps with the means to revolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PersianArmy_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-PersianArmy-58">[58]</a></sup> The order was however ignored by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Artabazus_of_Lydia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Artabazus of Lydia (page does not exist)">Artabazus of Lydia</a>, who asked for the help of Athens in a rebellion against the king. Athens sent assistance to <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orontid_dynasty#Orontids_Kings_and_satraps_of_Armenia" title="Orontid dynasty">Orontes of Mysia</a> also supported Artabazus and the combined forces managed to defeat the forces sent by Artaxerxes III in 354 BC. However, in 353 BC, they were defeated by Artaxerxes III's army and were disbanded. Orontes was pardoned by the king, while Artabazus fled to the safety of the court of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>. In around 351 BC, Artaxerxes embarked on a campaign to recover Egypt, which had revolted under his father, Artaxerxes II. At the same time a rebellion had broken out in Asia Minor, which, being supported by <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>, threatened to become serious. Levying a vast army, Artaxerxes marched into Egypt, and engaged <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a>. After a year of fighting the Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a>, Nectanebo inflicted a crushing defeat on the Persians with the support of mercenaries led by the Greek generals Diophantus and Lamius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Artaxerxes was compelled to retreat and postpone his plans to reconquer Egypt. Soon after this defeat, there were rebellions in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Phoenicia" title="Achaemenid Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Anatolia#Achaemenid_Empire" title="History of Anatolia">Asia Minor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history_of_Cyprus#Persian_period" title="Ancient history of Cyprus">Cyprus</a>. In 343 BC, Artaxerxes committed responsibility for the suppression of the Cyprian rebels to <a href="/wiki/Idrieus" title="Idrieus">Idrieus</a>, prince of <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, who employed 8,000 Greek mercenaries and forty <a href="/wiki/Trireme" title="Trireme">triremes</a>, commanded by <a href="/wiki/Phocion" title="Phocion">Phocion</a> the Athenian, and Evagoras, son of the elder <a href="/wiki/Evagoras_I" title="Evagoras I">Evagoras</a>, the Cypriot monarch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-61">[61]</a></sup> Idrieus succeeded in reducing Cyprus. Artaxerxes initiated a counter-offensive against <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a> by commanding Belesys, satrap of Syria and Mezseus, <a href="/wiki/Cilicia_(satrapy)" title="Cilicia (satrapy)">satrap of Cilicia</a> to invade the city and to keep the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> in check. Both satraps suffered crushing defeats at the hands of Tennes, the Sidonese king, who was aided by 40,000 Greek mercenaries sent to him by <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a> and commanded by <a href="/wiki/Mentor_of_Rhodes" title="Mentor of Rhodes">Mentor of Rhodes</a>. As a result, the Persian forces were driven out of <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>After this, Artaxerxes personally led an army of 330,000 men against <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>. Artaxerxes' army comprised 300,000 foot soldiers, 30,000 <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a>, 300 triremes, and 500 transports or provision ships. After gathering this army, he sought assistance from the Greeks. Though refused aid by <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, he succeeded in obtaining a thousand Theban heavy-armed hoplites under Lacrates, three thousand Argives under Nicostratus, and six thousand Æolians, <a href="/wiki/Ionians" title="Ionians">Ionians</a>, and Dorians from the Greek cities of Asia Minor. This Greek support was numerically small, amounting to no more than 10,000 men, but it formed, together with the Greek mercenaries from Egypt who went over to him afterwards, the force on which he placed his chief reliance, and to which the ultimate success of his expedition was mainly due. The approach of Artaxerxes sufficiently weakened the resolution of Tennes that he endeavoured to purchase his own pardon by delivering up 100 principal citizens of Sidon into the hands of the Persian king, and then admitting Artaxerxes within the defences of the town. Artaxerxes had the 100 citizens transfixed with javelins, and when 500 more came out as supplicants to seek his mercy, Artaxerxes consigned them to the same fate. Sidon was then burnt to the ground, either by Artaxerxes or by the Sidonian citizens. Forty thousand people died in the conflagration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-61">[61]</a></sup> Artaxerxes sold the ruins at a high price to speculators, who calculated on reimbursing themselves by the treasures which they hoped to dig out from among the ashes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Tennes was later put to death by Artaxerxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovetoknow_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lovetoknow-63">[63]</a></sup> Artaxerxes later sent Jews who supported the revolt to <a href="/wiki/Hyrcania" title="Hyrcania">Hyrcania</a> the south coast of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_conquest_of_Egypt">Second conquest of Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Second conquest of Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NectaneboII-StatueHead_MuseumOfFineArtsBoston.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1681" data-file-width="1071" height="345" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/NectaneboII-StatueHead_MuseumOfFineArtsBoston.png/220px-NectaneboII-StatueHead_MuseumOfFineArtsBoston.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/NectaneboII-StatueHead_MuseumOfFineArtsBoston.png/330px-NectaneboII-StatueHead_MuseumOfFineArtsBoston.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/NectaneboII-StatueHead_MuseumOfFineArtsBoston.png/440px-NectaneboII-StatueHead_MuseumOfFineArtsBoston.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NectaneboII-StatueHead_MuseumOfFineArtsBoston.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Head of <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:237px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Darius_II_cartouche_at_Hibis_d1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="943" data-file-width="629" height="352" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Darius_II_cartouche_at_Hibis_d1.jpg/235px-Darius_II_cartouche_at_Hibis_d1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Darius_II_cartouche_at_Hibis_d1.jpg/353px-Darius_II_cartouche_at_Hibis_d1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Darius_II_cartouche_at_Hibis_d1.jpg/470px-Darius_II_cartouche_at_Hibis_d1.jpg 2x" width="235"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Darius_II_cartouche_at_Hibis_d1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Cartouche" title="Cartouche">Cartouche</a> of <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Hibis" title="Temple of Hibis">Temple of Hibis</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The reduction of Sidon was followed closely by the invasion of Egypt. In 343 BC, Artaxerxes, in addition to his 330,000 Persians, had now a force of 14,000 Greeks furnished by the Greek cities of Asia Minor: 4,000 under <a href="/wiki/Mentor_of_Rhodes" title="Mentor of Rhodes">Mentor</a>, consisting of the troops which he had brought to the aid of Tennes from Egypt; 3,000 sent by Argos; and 1000 from Thebes. He divided these troops into three bodies, and placed at the head of each a Persian and a Greek. The Greek commanders were Lacrates of Thebes, <a href="/wiki/Mentor_of_Rhodes" title="Mentor of Rhodes">Mentor of Rhodes</a> and Nicostratus of Argos while the Persians were led by Rhossaces, Aristazanes, and <a href="/wiki/Bagoas" title="Bagoas">Bagoas</a>, the chief of the eunuchs. <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a> resisted with an army of 100,000 of whom 20,000 were Greek mercenaries. Nectanebo II occupied the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> and its various branches with his large navy.</p>
<p>The character of the country, intersected by numerous canals and full of strongly fortified towns, was in his favour and Nectanebo II might have been expected to offer a prolonged, if not even a successful, resistance. However, he lacked good generals, and, over-confident in his own powers of command, he was out-manoeuvred by the Greek mercenary generals and his forces eventually defeated by the combined Persian armies at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pelusium_(343_BC)" title="Battle of Pelusium (343 BC)">Battle of Pelusium (343 BC)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-61">[61]</a></sup> After his defeat, Nectanebo hastily fled to <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>, leaving the fortified towns to be defended by their garrisons. These garrisons consisted of partly <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> and partly Egyptian troops; between whom jealousies and suspicions were easily sown by the Persian leaders. As a result, the Persians were able to rapidly reduce numerous towns across Lower Egypt and were advancing upon Memphis when Nectanebo decided to quit the country and flee southwards to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-61">[61]</a></sup> The Persian army completely routed the Egyptians and occupied the Lower Delta of the Nile. Following Nectanebo fleeing to Ethiopia, all of Egypt submitted to Artaxerxes. The Jews in Egypt were sent either to <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> or to the south coast of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>, the same location that the Jews of <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> had earlier been sent.</p>
<p>After this victory over the Egyptians, Artaxerxes had the city walls destroyed, started a reign of terror, and set about looting all the temples. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> gained a significant amount of wealth from this looting. Artaxerxes also raised high taxes and attempted to weaken <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> enough that it could never revolt against Persia. For the 10 years that Persia controlled Egypt, believers in the native religion were persecuted and sacred books were stolen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Egyptloot_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Egyptloot-66">[66]</a></sup> Before he returned to Persia, he appointed Pherendares as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Achaemenid_Egypt" title="History of Achaemenid Egypt">satrap of Egypt</a>. With the wealth gained from his reconquering Egypt, Artaxerxes was able to amply reward his mercenaries. He then returned to his capital having successfully completed his invasion of Egypt.</p>
<p>After his success in Egypt, Artaxerxes returned to Persia and spent the next few years effectively quelling insurrections in various parts of the Empire so that a few years after his conquest of Egypt, the Persian Empire was firmly under his control. Egypt remained a part of the Persian Empire until <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>'s conquest of Egypt.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1035" data-file-width="1359" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Achaemenid_Empire.gif/350px-Achaemenid_Empire.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Achaemenid_Empire.gif/525px-Achaemenid_Empire.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Achaemenid_Empire.gif/700px-Achaemenid_Empire.gif 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Persian Empire timeline including important events and territorial evolution.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the conquest of Egypt, there were no more revolts or rebellions against Artaxerxes. Mentor and <a href="/wiki/Bagoas" title="Bagoas">Bagoas</a>, the two generals who had most distinguished themselves in the Egyptian campaign, were advanced to posts of the highest importance. Mentor, who was governor of the entire Asiatic seaboard, was successful in reducing to subjection many of the chiefs who during the recent troubles had rebelled against Persian rule. In the course of a few years Mentor and his forces were able to bring the whole Asian Mediterranean coast into complete submission and dependence.</p>
<p>Bagoas went back to the Persian capital with Artaxerxes, where he took a leading role in the internal administration of the Empire and maintained tranquility throughout the rest of the Empire. During the last six years of the reign of Artaxerxes III, the Persian Empire was governed by a vigorous and successful government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_61-5"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Persian forces in <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycia</a> regained control of the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and took over much of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>' former island empire. In response, <a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">Isocrates</a> of Athens started giving speeches calling for a 'crusade against the barbarians' but there was not enough strength left in any of the Greek city-states to answer his call.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conspiracy_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-conspiracy-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although there weren't any rebellions in the Persian Empire itself, the growing power and territory of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> (against which <a href="/wiki/Demosthenes" title="Demosthenes">Demosthenes</a> was in vain warning the Athenians) attracted the attention of Artaxerxes. In response, he ordered that Persian influence was to be used to check and constrain the rising power and influence of the Macedonian kingdom. In 340 BC, a Persian force was dispatched to assist the <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thracian prince</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cersobleptes" title="Cersobleptes">Cersobleptes</a>, to maintain his independence. Sufficient effective aid was given to the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perinthus" title="Perinthus">Perinthus</a> that the numerous and well-appointed army with which Philip had commenced his siege of the city was compelled to give up the attempt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persianempire_61-6"><a href="#cite_note-Persianempire-61">[61]</a></sup> By the last year of Artaxerxes' rule, Philip II already had plans in place for an invasion of the Persian Empire, which would crown his career, but the Greeks would not unite with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 338 BC Artaxerxes was poisoned by <a href="/wiki/Bagoas" title="Bagoas">Bagoas</a> with the assistance of a physician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_of_the_empire">Fall of the empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fall of the empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2133" data-file-width="3562" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg/220px-Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg/330px-Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg/440px-Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Battle of Issus</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> on horseback to the left, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_III_of_Persia" title="Darius III of Persia">Darius III</a> in the chariot to the right, represented in a Pompeii <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> dated 1st century BC – <a href="/wiki/Naples_National_Archaeological_Museum" title="Naples National Archaeological Museum">Naples National Archaeological Museum</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius,_the_Persian_king.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="539" data-file-width="800" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg/220px-Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg/330px-Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg/440px-Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius%2C_the_Persian_king.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander%E2%80%99s_first_victory_over_Darius,_the_Persian_king.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander's first victory over Darius, the Persian king depicted in medieval European style in the 15th century romance <i>The History of Alexander's Battles</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Artaxerxes III was succeeded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_IV_Arses" title="Artaxerxes IV Arses">Artaxerxes IV Arses</a>, who before he could act was also poisoned by Bagoas. Bagoas is further said to have killed not only all Arses' children, but many of the other princes of the land. Bagoas then placed <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a>, a nephew of Artaxerxes IV, on the throne. Darius III, previously <a href="/wiki/Orontid_Dynasty" title="Orontid Dynasty">Satrap of Armenia</a>, personally forced Bagoas to swallow poison. In 334 BC, when Darius was just succeeding in subduing Egypt again, Alexander and his battle-hardened troops <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">invaded Asia Minor</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> (Alexander III of Macedon) defeated the Persian armies at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Granicus" title="Battle of Granicus">Granicus</a> (334 BC), followed by <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Issus</a> (333 BC), and lastly at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Gaugamela</a> (331 BC). Afterwards, he marched on <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> which surrendered in early 330 BC. From Persepolis, Alexander headed north to Pasargadae where he visited the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Cyrus" title="Tomb of Cyrus">tomb of Cyrus</a>, the burial of the man whom he had heard of from the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyropedia" title="Cyropedia">Cyropedia</a></i>.</p>
<p>In the ensuing chaos created by Alexander's invasion of Persia, Cyrus's tomb was broken into and most of its luxuries were looted. When Alexander reached the tomb, he was horrified by the manner in which it had been treated, and questioned the Magi, putting them on trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleveland_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cleveland-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> By some accounts, Alexander's decision to put the Magi on trial was more an attempt to undermine their influence and display his own power than a show of concern for Cyrus's tomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Regardless, Alexander the Great ordered Aristobulus to improve the tomb's condition and restore its interior, showing respect for Cyrus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleveland_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cleveland-70">[70]</a></sup> From there he headed to <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>, where Darius III had sought refuge.</p>
<p>Darius III was taken prisoner by <a href="/wiki/Bessus" title="Bessus">Bessus</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> and kinsman. As Alexander approached, Bessus had his men murder Darius III and then declared himself Darius' successor, as Artaxerxes V, before retreating into Central Asia leaving Darius' body in the road to delay Alexander, who brought it to Persepolis for an honorable funeral. Bessus would then create a coalition of his forces, in order to create an army to defend against Alexander. Before Bessus could fully unite with his confederates at the eastern part of the empire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Alexander, fearing the danger of Bessus gaining control, found him, put him on trial in a Persian court under his control, and ordered his execution in a "cruel and barbarous manner".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander generally kept the original Achaemenid administrative structure, leading some scholars to dub him as "the last of the Achaemenids"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Upon Alexander's death in 323 BC, his empire was divided among his generals, the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a>, resulting in a number of smaller states. The largest of these, which held sway over the Iranian plateau, was <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, ruled by Alexander's general <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a>. Native Iranian rule would be restored by the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> of northeastern Iran over the course of the 2nd century BC.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descendants_in_later_Iranian_dynasties">Descendants in later Iranian dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Descendants in later Iranian dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Istakhr" title="Istakhr">Istakhr</a>, one of the vassal kingdoms of the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>, would be overthrown by Papak, a priest of the temple there. Papak's son, Ardašir I, who named himself in remembrance of Artaxerxes II, would revolt against the Parthians, eventually defeating them and establishing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empire</a> or as it is known the second Persian Empire.</p>
<p>The Achaemenid line would be carried on through the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Pontus" title="Kingdom of Pontus">Kingdom of Pontus</a>, based in the <a href="/wiki/Pontus_(region)" title="Pontus (region)">Pontus</a> region of northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. This Pontic Kingdom, a state of <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranicaonline.org_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-iranicaonline.org-79">[79]</a></sup> may even have been directly related to <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius the Great</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_dynasty" title="Achaemenid dynasty">Achaemenid dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranicaonline.org_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-iranicaonline.org-79">[79]</a></sup> It was founded by <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_I_of_Pontus" title="Mithridates I of Pontus">Mithridates I</a> in 281 BC and lasted until its conquest by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> in 63 BC. The kingdom grew to its largest extent under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mithridates_VI" title="Mithridates VI">Mithridates VI</a> the Great, who conquered <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a>, the Greek colonies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chersonesus_Taurica" title="Chersonesus Taurica">Tauric Chersonesos</a> and for a brief time the Roman province of <a href="/wiki/Asia_(Roman_province)" title="Asia (Roman province)">Asia</a>. Thus, this Persian dynasty managed to survive and prosper in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_world" title="Hellenistic world">Hellenistic world</a> while the main Persian Empire had fallen.</p>
<p>Both the later dynasties of the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanians</a> would on occasion claim Achaemenid descent. Recently there has been some corroboration for the Parthian claim to Achaemenid ancestry via the possibility of an inherited disease (neurofibromatosis) demonstrated by the physical descriptions of rulers and from evidence of familial disease on ancient coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_decline">Causes of decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Causes of decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Part of the cause of the Empire's decline had been the heavy tax burden put upon the state, which eventually led to economic decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> An estimate of the tribute imposed on the subject nations was up to U.S. $180M per year. This does not include the material goods and supplies that were supplied as taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_p.363_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_p.363-83">[83]</a></sup> After the high overhead of government - the military, the bureaucracy, whatever the satraps could safely dip into the coffers for themselves - this money went into the royal treasury. According to Diodorus, at Persepolis, Alexander III found some 180,000 <a href="/wiki/Attic_talent" title="Attic talent">Attic talents</a> of silver besides the additional treasure the Macedonians were carrying that already had been seized in Damascus by <a href="/wiki/Parmenion" title="Parmenion">Parmenion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='"citation'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This amounted to U.S. $2.7B. On top of this, Darius III had taken 8,000 talents with him on his flight to the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_p.363_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_p.363-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='"citation'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Alexander put this static hoard back into the economy, and upon his death some 130,000 talents had been spent on the building of cities, dockyards, temples, and the payment of the troops, besides the ordinary government expenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='"citation'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Additionally, one of the satraps, Harpalus, had made off to Greece with some 6,000 talents, which Athens used to rebuild its economy after seizing it during the struggles with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corinthian_League" title="Corinthian League">Corinthian League</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='"citation'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Due to the flood of money from Alexander's hoard entering Greece, however, a disruption in the economy occurred, in agriculture, banking, rents, the great increase in mercenary soldiers that cash allowed the wealthy, and an increase in piracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title='"citation'>better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Another factor contributing to the decline of the Empire after Xerxes was its failure to ever mold the many subject nations into a whole; the creation of a national identity was never attempted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> This lack of cohesion eventually affected the efficiency of the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg/220px-Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg/330px-Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg/440px-Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Darius_I_the_Great%27s_inscription.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a> tells the story of <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius the Great's</a> conquests, with the names of twenty-three <i>satrapys</i> subject to him.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Behistun_DB1_1-15.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="331" data-file-width="1000" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Behistun_DB1_1-15.jpg/220px-Behistun_DB1_1-15.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Behistun_DB1_1-15.jpg/330px-Behistun_DB1_1-15.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Behistun_DB1_1-15.jpg/440px-Behistun_DB1_1-15.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
