</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cylinder_Seal,_Achaemenid,_modern_impression_05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="891" data-file-width="1493" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cylinder_Seal%2C_Achaemenid%2C_modern_impression_05.jpg/220px-Cylinder_Seal%2C_Achaemenid%2C_modern_impression_05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cylinder_Seal%2C_Achaemenid%2C_modern_impression_05.jpg/330px-Cylinder_Seal%2C_Achaemenid%2C_modern_impression_05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cylinder_Seal%2C_Achaemenid%2C_modern_impression_05.jpg/440px-Cylinder_Seal%2C_Achaemenid%2C_modern_impression_05.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cylinder_Seal,_Achaemenid,_modern_impression_05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Modern impression of Achaemenid <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_seal" title="Cylinder seal">cylinder seal</a>. The use of cylinder seals appears to have been restricted to officials of the royal administration during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> founded the empire as a multi-<a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> empire, governed by four capital states; <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ekbatana" title="Ekbatana">Ekbatana</a>. The Achaemenids allowed a certain amount of regional autonomy in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrapy</a> system. A satrapy was an administrative unit, usually organized on a geographical basis. A '<a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a>' (governor) was the governor who administered the region, a 'general' supervised military recruitment and ensured order, and a 'state secretary' kept the official records. The general and the state secretary reported directly to the satrap as well as the central government. At differing times, there were between 20 and 30 satrapies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cyrus the Great created an organized army including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Immortals" title="Persian Immortals">Immortals</a> unit, consisting of 10,000 highly trained soldiers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-army_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-army-92">[92]</a></sup> Cyrus also formed an innovative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postal_system" title="Postal system">postal system</a> throughout the empire, based on several relay stations called <a href="/wiki/Chapar_Khaneh" title="Chapar Khaneh">Chapar Khaneh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Persian <b>daric</b> was the first <a href="/wiki/Gold_coin" title="Gold coin">gold coin</a> which, along with a similar silver coin, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siglos" title="Siglos">siglos</a>, (From <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> σίγλος, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> שֶׁקֶל (<a href="/wiki/Shekel" title="Shekel">shékel</a>)) introduced the bimetallic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monetary_standard" title="Monetary standard">monetary standard</a> of the Achaemenid Persian Empire which has continued till today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranica_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-iranica-94">[94]</a></sup> This was accomplished by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>, who reinforced the empire and expanded <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> as a ceremonial capital;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> he revolutionized the economy by placing it on the silver and gold coinage and introducing a regulated and sustainable tax system that was precisely tailored to each satrapy, based on their supposed productivity and their economic potential. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> was assessed for the highest amount and for a startling mixture of commodities – 1000 <a href="/wiki/Talent_(measurement)" title="Talent (measurement)">silver talents</a>, four months supply of food for the army. <a href="/wiki/Hindush" title="Hindush">India</a> was clearly already fabled for its gold; <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> was known for the wealth of its crops; it was to be the granary of the Persian Empire (as later of Rome's) and was required to provide 120,000 measures of grain in addition to 700 talents of silver. This was exclusively a tax levied on subject peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historyworld.net_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-historyworld.net-96">[96]</a></sup> Other accomplishments of Darius' reign included <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codification</a> of the data, a universal legal system, and construction of a new capital at Persepolis.</p>
<p>Under the Achaemenids, the trade was extensive and there was an efficient infrastructure that facilitated the exchange of commodities in the far reaches of the empire. Tariffs on trade were one of the empire's main sources of revenue, along with agriculture and tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historyworld.net_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-historyworld.net-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>The satrapies were linked by a 2,500-kilometer highway, the most impressive stretch being the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Road" title="Royal Road">Royal Road</a> from <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>, built by command of Darius I. The relays of mounted couriers could reach the remotest of areas in fifteen days. Herodotus observes that "there is nothing in the world that travels faster than these Persian couriers. Neither snow, nor rain, nor heat, nor gloom of night stays these courageous couriers from the swift completion of their appointed rounds."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite the relative local independence afforded by the satrapy system, royal inspectors, the "eyes and ears of the king", toured the empire and reported on local conditions.</p>
<p>The practice of slavery in Achaemenid Persia was generally banned, although there is evidence that conquered and/or rebellious armies were sold into captivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The kings of Achaemenid Persia, especially the founder <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, occasionally declined to adopt slavery, as evidenced by the freeing of the Jews at Babylon, and the construction of Persepolis by paid workers.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="418" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg/220px-Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg/330px-Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Median (left) and Persian (right) soldiers</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite its humble origins in Persis, the empire reached an enormous size under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>. Cyrus created a multi-state empire where he allowed regional rulers, called the '<a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a>' to rule as his proxy over a certain designated area of his empire called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satrapy" title="Satrapy">satrapy</a>. The basic rule of governance was based upon loyalty and obedience of each satrapy to the central power, or the king, and compliance with tax laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyv_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-cyv-22">[22]</a></sup> Due to the ethnocultural diversity of the subject nations under the rule of Persia, its enormous geographic size, and the constant struggle for power by regional competitors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-book-14">[14]</a></sup> the creation of a professional army was necessary for both maintenance of the peace, and also to enforce the authority of the king in cases of rebellion and foreign threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmitt-EI-i_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-schmitt-EI-i-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-army_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-army-92">[92]</a></sup> Cyrus managed to create a strong land army, using it to advance in his campaigns in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_empire" title="Babylonian empire">Babylonia</a>, Lydia, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, which after his death was used by his son <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> against <a href="/wiki/Psamtik_III" title="Psamtik III">Psamtik III</a>. Cyrus would die battling a local Iranian insurgency in the empire, before he could have a chance to develop a naval force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> That task however would fall to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>, who would officially give Persians their own royal navy to allow them to engage their enemies on multiple seas of this vast empire, from the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Sea" title="Ionian Sea">Ionian Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_composition">Military composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Military composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The empire's great armies were, like the empire itself, very diverse, having:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[note 1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-84_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-84-102">[101]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_37-8"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-37">[37]</a></sup> European <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thracians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paionia" title="Paionia">Paeonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, Achaean <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khuzistan" title="Khuzistan">Cissians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyrcanians" title="Hyrcanians">Hyrcanians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-62_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-62-103">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-63_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-63-104">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacae" title="Sacae">Sacae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-64_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-64-105">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aria_(satrapy)" title="Aria (satrapy)">Arians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_Albania" title="Caucasian Albania">Caucasian Albanians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaumont_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaumont-106">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Khwarezm" title="Khwarezm">Chorasmians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandarians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daradas" title="Daradas">Dadicae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-66_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-66-107">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caspians" title="Caspians">Caspians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drangiana" title="Drangiana">Sarangae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashtun_people" title="Pashtun people">Pactyes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-67_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-67-108">[107]</a></sup> Utians, <a href="/wiki/Maka_(satrapy)" title="Maka (satrapy)">Mycians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> along with the "Syrians of <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>" (likely Judeans), <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii.3D89_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii.3D89-109">[108]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_Cypriots" title="Greek Cypriots">Cyprians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-90_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-90-110">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pamphylia" title="Pamphylia">Pamphylians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">Dorians</a> of Asia, <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Carians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Islands" title="Aegean Islands">Aegean islanders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aeolis" title="Aeolis">Aeolians</a>, Greeks from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontus" title="Pontus">Pontus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balochistan_(Pakistan)" title="Balochistan (Pakistan)">Paricanians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-68_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-68-111">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopians of Africa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-69_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-69-112">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sistan_and_Baluchestan_Province" title="Sistan and Baluchestan Province">Ethiopians of Baluchistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-70_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-70-113">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libyans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-71_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-71-114">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paphlagonia" title="Paphlagonia">Paphlagonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kutaisi" title="Kutaisi">Ligyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matiene" title="Matiene">Matieni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-73_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-73-116">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mysia" title="Mysia">Mysians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-74_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-74-117">[116]</a></sup> Asian <a href="/wiki/Bithyni" title="Bithyni">Thracians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-75_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-75-118">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pisidia" title="Pisidia">Lasonii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Milyae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-77_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-77-119">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mushki" title="Mushki">Moschi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tabal" title="Tabal">Tibareni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macrones" title="Macrones">Macrones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mossynoeci" title="Mossynoeci">Mossynoeci</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-78_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-78-120">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Mares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">Alarodians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> islanders,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-80_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-80-122">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sagartians" title="Sagartians">Sagartians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-85_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-85-123">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-65_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-vii-65-124">[123]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eordea" title="Eordea">Eordi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bottiaea" title="Bottiaea">Bottiaei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chalkidiki" title="Chalkidiki">Chalcidians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bryges" title="Bryges">Brygians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pieres" title="Pieres">Pierians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perrhaebi" title="Perrhaebi">Perrhaebi</a>, Enienes, Dolopes, and <a href="/wiki/Magnesia_Prefecture" title="Magnesia Prefecture">Magnesians</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infantry">Infantry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Infantry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Achaemenid infantry consisted of three groups: the <a href="/wiki/Immortals_(Persian_Empire)" title="Immortals (Persian Empire)">Immortals</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sparabara" title="Sparabara">Sparabara</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Takabara" title="Takabara">Takabara</a>, though in the later years of the Achaemenid Empire, the <a href="/wiki/Cardaces" title="Cardaces">Cardaces</a>, were introduced.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immortals_(Persian_empire)" title="Immortals (Persian empire)">Immortals</a> were described by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> as being heavy <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Hydarnes_II" title="Hydarnes II">Hydarnes</a>, that were kept constantly at a strength of exactly 10,000 men. He claimed that the unit's name stemmed from the custom that every killed, seriously wounded, or sick member was immediately replaced with a new one, maintaining the numbers and cohesion of the unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> They had wicker shields, short spears, swords or large daggers, bow and arrow. Underneath their robes they wore <a href="/wiki/Scale_armour" title="Scale armour">scale armour</a> coats. The spear counterbalances of the common soldiery were of silver; to differentiate commanding ranks, the officers' spear butt-spikes were golden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius.org2_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius.org2-126">[125]</a></sup> Surviving Achaemenid coloured glazed bricks and carved reliefs represent the Immortals as wearing elaborate robes, hoop earrings and gold jewelry, though these garments and accessories were most likely worn only for ceremonial occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sparabara" title="Sparabara">Sparabara</a> unit were usually the first to engage in hand-to-hand combat with the enemy. Although not much is known about them today, it is believed that they were the backbone of the Persian army who formed a <a href="/wiki/Shield_wall" title="Shield wall">shield wall</a> and used their two-metre-long spears to protect more vulnerable troops such as <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archers</a> from the enemy. The Sparabara were taken from the full members of Persian society, they were trained from childhood to be soldiers and when not called out to fight on campaigns in distant lands they practised hunting on the vast plains of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>. However, when all was quiet and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pax_Persica&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pax Persica (page does not exist)">Pax Persica</a> held true, the <i>Sparabara</i> returned to normal life farming the land and grazing their herds. Because of this they lacked true professional quality on the battlefield, yet they were well trained and courageous to the point of holding the line in most situations long enough for a counterattack. They were armoured with quilted <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a> and carried large rectangular <a href="/wiki/Wicker" title="Wicker">wicker</a> shields as a form of light manoeuvrable defense. This, however, left them at a severe disadvantage against heavily armoured opponents such as the <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplite</a>, and his two-metre-long spear was not able to give the Sparabara ample range to plausibly engage a trained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalanx_formation" title="Phalanx formation">phalanx</a>. The wicker shields were able to effectively stop arrows but not strong enough to protect the soldier from spears. However, the Sparabara could deal with most other infantry, including trained units from the East. The <a href="/wiki/Takabara" title="Takabara">Takabara</a> were a rare unit who were a tough type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peltasts" title="Peltasts">peltasts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> Takabara nevertheless were more garrison warriors than front line fighters as proved against the well-armed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoplites" title="Hoplites">Hoplites</a> of Greece where they were easily defeated in hand to hand conflict.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> They tended to fight with their own native weapons which would have included a crescent-shaped light wickerwork <a href="/wiki/Shield" title="Shield">shield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Axe" title="Axe">axes</a> as well as light linen cloth and <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a>. The Takabara were recruited from territories that incorporated modern Iraq and parts of Iran.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cavalry">Cavalry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cavalry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">The armoured Persian horsemen and their death dealing chariots were invincible.
<p>No man dared face them</p>
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="cquotecite" colspan="3" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; text-align: right;"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Persian Cavalry was crucial for conquering nations, and had maintained its importance in the Achaemenid army to the last days of the Achaemenid Empire. The cavalry were separated into four groups. The Chariot Archers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_cavalry" title="Horse cavalry">Horse cavalry</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Camel_cavalry" title="Camel cavalry">Camel cavalry</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Persian_war_elephants" title="Persian war elephants">Elephant Cavalry</a>.</p>
<p>In the later years of the Achaemenid Empire, the Chariot archer had become merely a ceremonial part of the Persian army, yet in the early years of the Empire, their use was widespread. The Chariot archers were armed with Spears, Bows, Arrows, Swords, and <a href="/wiki/Scale_armour" title="Scale armour">scale armour</a>. The horses were also suited with <a href="/wiki/Scale_armour" title="Scale armour">scale armour</a> similar to scale armour of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanian" title="Sassanian">Sassanian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">Cataphracts</a>. The Chariots would contain imperial symbols and Decorations. The <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">Horses</a> used by the Achaemenids for Cavalry were often suited with <a href="/wiki/Scale_armour" title="Scale armour">scale armour</a>, like most cavalry units. The riders often had the same armour as Infantry units, wicker shields, short spears, swords or large daggers, bow and arrow and <a href="/wiki/Scale_armour" title="Scale armour">scale armour</a> coats. The <a href="/wiki/Camel_cavalry" title="Camel cavalry">Camel cavalry</a> was different, because the Camels and sometimes the riders, were provided little protection against enemies, yet when they were offered protection, they would have Spears, Swords, Bow, Arrow, and <a href="/wiki/Scale_armour" title="Scale armour">scale armour</a>. The Persian Camel Cavalry was first introduced into the Persian army by <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thymbra" title="Battle of Thymbra">battle of Thymbra</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_war_elephant" title="Persian war elephant">Persian war elephant</a> was most likely introduced into the Persian army by <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> after his <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_invasion_of_the_Indus_Valley" title="Achaemenid invasion of the Indus Valley">conquest of the Indus Valley</a>. They may have been used in Greek campaigns by Darius and <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>, but Greek accounts only mention 15 of them being used at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Battle of Gaugamela</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navy">Navy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Navy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since its foundation by Cyrus, the Persian empire had been primarily a land empire with a strong army, but void of any actual naval forces. By the 5th century BC, this was to change, as the empire came across Greek, and Egyptian forces, each with their own maritime traditions and capabilities. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> (Darius I) is to be credited as the first Achaemenid king to invest in a Persian fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-navy-129">[128]</a></sup> Even by then no true "imperial navy" had existed either in Greece or Egypt. Persia would become the first empire, under Darius, to inaugurate and deploy the first regular imperial navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-navy-129">[128]</a></sup> Despite this achievement, the personnel for the imperial navy would not come from Iran, but were often <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> (mostly from <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>), <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_people" title="Greek people">Greeks</a> chosen by Darius the Great to operate the empire's combat vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-navy-129">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>At first the ships were built in Sidon by the Phoenicians; the first Achaemenid ships measured about 40 meters in length and 6 meters in width, able to transport up to 300 Persian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Immortals" title="Persian Immortals">troops</a> at any one trip. Soon, other states of the empire were constructing their own ships, each incorporating slight local preferences. The ships eventually found their way to the Persian Gulf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_129-3"><a href="#cite_note-navy-129">[128]</a></sup> Persian naval forces laid the foundation for a strong Persian maritime presence in the Persian Gulf. Persians were not only stationed on islands in the Persian Gulf, but also had ships often of 100 to 200 capacity patrolling the empire's various rivers including the Caroon or Karun ( Arabic name:<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shatt-al-Arab" title="Shatt-al-Arab">Shatt-al-Arab</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tigris_river" title="Tigris river">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> in the west, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_129-4"><a href="#cite_note-navy-129">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Achaemenid navy established bases located along the Shatt-al-Arab, Bahrain, Oman, and Yemen. The Persian fleet was not only used for peace-keeping purposes along the Shatt al-Arab but also opened the door to trade with India via the Persian Gulf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_129-5"><a href="#cite_note-navy-129">[128]</a></sup> Darius's navy was in many ways a world power at the time, but it would be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> who in the summer of 397 BC would build a formidable navy, as part of a rearmament which would lead to his decisive victory at <a href="/wiki/Knidos" title="Knidos">Knidos</a> in 394 BC, reestablishing Achaemenid power in <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>. Artaxerxes II would also utilize his navy to later on quell a rebellion in Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navi_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-navi-130">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>The construction material of choice was wood, but some armored Achaemenid ships had metallic blades on the front, often meant to slice enemy ships using the ship's momentum. Naval ships were also equipped with hooks on the side to grab enemy ships, or to negotiate their position. The ships were propelled by sails or manpower. The ships the Persians created were unique. As far as maritime engagement, the ships were equipped with two <a href="/wiki/Mangonel" title="Mangonel">mangonels</a> that would launch projectiles such as stones, or flammable substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_129-6"><a href="#cite_note-navy-129">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> describes his eye-witness account of a massive military bridge created by joining 37 Persian ships across the Tigris river. The Persians utilized each boat's buoyancy, in order to support a connected bridge above which supply could be transferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navy_129-7"><a href="#cite_note-navy-129">[128]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> also gives many accounts of Persians utilizing ships to build bridges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> Darius the Great, in an attempt to subdue the <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythian</a> horsemen north of the Black Sea, crossed over at the <a href="/wiki/Bosphorus" title="Bosphorus">Bosphorus</a>, using an enormous bridge made by connecting Achaemenid boats, then marched up to the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, crossing it by means of a second boat bridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> The bridge over the Bosphorus essentially connected the nearest tip of Asia to Europe, encompassing at least some 1000 meters of open water if not more. Herodotus describes the spectacle, and calls it the "bridge of Darius":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"<i>Strait called Bosphorus, across which the bridge of Darius had been thrown is hundred and twenty <a href="/wiki/Furlong" title="Furlong">furlongs</a> in length, reaching from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euxine" title="Euxine">Euxine</a>, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propontis" title="Propontis">Propontis</a>. The Propontis is five hundred furlongs across, and fourteen hundred long. Its waters flow into the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Hellespont</a>, the length of which is four hundred furlongs ...</i>"</dd>
</dl>
<p>Years later, a similar boat bridge would be constructed by Xerxes the Great (<a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>), in his invasion of Greece. Although the Persians failed to capture the Greek city states completely, the tradition of maritime involvement was carried down by the Persian kings, most notably Artaxerxes II. Years later, when Alexander invaded Persia and during his advancement into India, he took a page from the Persian art of war, by having <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a> construct a similar boat-bridge at the Indus river, in India in spring of 327 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Armlet_from_the_Oxus_Treasure_BM_1897.12-31.116.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1781" data-file-width="1782" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Armlet_from_the_Oxus_Treasure_BM_1897.12-31.116.jpg/220px-Armlet_from_the_Oxus_Treasure_BM_1897.12-31.116.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Armlet_from_the_Oxus_Treasure_BM_1897.12-31.116.jpg/330px-Armlet_from_the_Oxus_Treasure_BM_1897.12-31.116.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Armlet_from_the_Oxus_Treasure_BM_1897.12-31.116.jpg/440px-Armlet_from_the_Oxus_Treasure_BM_1897.12-31.116.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Armlet_from_the_Oxus_Treasure_BM_1897.12-31.116.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of a pair of armlets from the <a href="/wiki/Oxus_Treasure" title="Oxus Treasure">Oxus Treasure</a>, which has lost its inlays of precious gems or enamel</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gold_cup_kalardasht.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="640" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Gold_cup_kalardasht.jpg/220px-Gold_cup_kalardasht.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Gold_cup_kalardasht.jpg/330px-Gold_cup_kalardasht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Gold_cup_kalardasht.jpg/440px-Gold_cup_kalardasht.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gold_cup_kalardasht.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Achaemenid golden bowl with lion imagery.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Persepolis001.jpg/220px-Persepolis001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Persepolis001.jpg/330px-Persepolis001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Persepolis001.jpg/440px-Persepolis001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The ruins of Persepolis</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, in his mid-5th century BC account of Persian residents of the Pontus, reports that Persian youths, from their fifth year to their twentieth year, were instructed in three things – <i>to ride a horse, to draw a bow, and to speak the Truth</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hero_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-hero-136">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>He further notes that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hero_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-hero-136">[135]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>the most disgraceful thing in the world [the Persians] think, is to tell a lie; the next worst, to owe a debt: because, among other reasons, the debtor is obliged to tell lies.</dd>
</dl>
<p>In Achaemenid Persia, the lie, <i>druj</i>, is considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Seven_deadly_sins" title="Seven deadly sins">cardinal sin</a>, and it was punishable by death in some extreme cases. Tablets discovered by archaeologists in the 1930s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIP_117_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-OIP_117-137">[136]</a></sup> at the site of Persepolis give us adequate evidence about the love and veneration for the culture of truth during the Achaemenian period. These tablets contain the names of ordinary Persians, mainly traders and warehouse-keepers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIR_Dandamayev_Elamite_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIR_Dandamayev_Elamite-138">[137]</a></sup> According to Professor Stanley Insler of <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>, as many as 72 names of officials and petty clerks found on these tablets contain the word <i>truth</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> Thus, says Insler, we have <i>Artapana</i>, protector of truth, <i>Artakama</i>, lover of truth, <i>Artamanah</i>, truth-minded, <i>Artafarnah</i>, possessing splendour of truth, <i>Artazusta</i>, delighting in truth, <i>Artastuna</i>, pillar of truth, <i>Artafrida</i>, prospering the truth and <i>Artahunara</i>, having nobility of truth. It was Darius the Great who laid down the <i>ordinance of good regulations</i> during his reign. King Darius' testimony about his constant battle against the lie is found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> inscriptions. Carved high up in the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun</a> mountain on the road to <a href="/wiki/Kermanshah" title="Kermanshah">Kermanshah</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> (Darius I) testifies:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>I was not a lie-follower, I was not a doer of wrong ... According to righteousness I conducted myself. Neither to the weak or to the powerful did I do wrong. The man who cooperated with my house, him I rewarded well; who so did injury, him I punished well.</dd>
</dl>
<p>Darius had his hands full dealing with large-scale rebellion which broke out throughout the empire. After fighting successfully with nine traitors in a year, Darius records his battles against them for posterity and tells us how it was the <i>lie</i> that made them rebel against the empire. At Behistun, Darius says:</p>
<dl>
<dd>I smote them and took prisoner nine kings. One was Gaumata by name, a Magian; he lied; thus he said: I am Smerdis, the son of Cyrus ... One, Acina by name, an Elamite; he lied; thus he said: I am king in Elam ... One, Nidintu-Bel by name, a Babylonian; he lied; thus he said: I am Nebuchadnezzar, the son of Nabonidus.</dd>
</dl>
<p>King Darius then tells us,</p>
<dl>
<dd>The Lie made them rebellious, so that these men deceived the people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Then advice to his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerxes_I_of_Persia" title="Xerxes I of Persia">Xerxes</a>, who is to succeed him as the great king:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Thou who shalt be king hereafter, protect yourself vigorously from the Lie; the man who shall be a lie-follower, him do thou punish well, if thus thou shall think. May my country be secure!</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the reign of Cyrus and Darius, and as long as the seat of government was still at <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a>, the language of the chancellory was <a href="/wiki/Elamite_language" title="Elamite language">Elamite</a>. This is primarily attested in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persepolis_Fortification_Tablets" title="Persepolis Fortification Tablets">Persepolis fortification and treasury tablets</a> that reveal details of the day-to-day functioning of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIR_Dandamayev_Elamite_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-EIR_Dandamayev_Elamite-138">[137]</a></sup> In the grand rock-face inscriptions of the kings, the Elamite texts are always accompanied by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> (Babylonian dialect) and <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> inscriptions, and it appears that in these cases, the Elamite texts are translations of the Old Persian ones. It is then likely that although Elamite was used by the capital government in Susa, it was not a standardized language of government everywhere in the empire. The use of Elamite is not attested after 458 BC.</p>
<p>Following the conquest of Mesopotamia, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Official_Aramaic" title="Official Aramaic">Aramaic</a> language (as used in that territory) was adopted as a "vehicle for written communication between the different regions of the vast empire with its different peoples and languages. The use of a single official language, which modern scholarship has dubbed "Official Aramaic" or "Imperial Aramaic", can be assumed to have greatly contributed to the astonishing success of the Achaemenids in holding their far-flung empire together for as long as they did."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> In 1955, Richard Frye questioned the classification of Imperial Aramaic as an "<a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>", noting that no surviving edict expressly and unambiguously accorded that status to any particular language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> Frye reclassifies Imperial Aramaic as the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the Achaemenid territories, suggesting then that the Achaemenid-era use of Aramaic was more pervasive than generally thought. Many centuries after the fall of the empire, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic_script" title="Aramaic script">Aramaic script</a> and – as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huzvarishn" title="Huzvarishn">ideograms</a> – Aramaic vocabulary would survive as the essential characteristics of the <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_scripts" title="Pahlavi scripts">Pahlavi writing system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Old Persian also appears on some seals and art objects, that language is attested primarily in the Achaemenid inscriptions of Western Iran, suggesting then that Old Persian was the common language of that region. However, by the reign of Artaxerxes II, the grammar and orthography of the inscriptions was so "far from perfect"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> that it has been suggested that the scribes who composed those texts had already largely forgotten the language, and had to rely on older inscriptions, which they to a great extent reproduced verbatim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the occasion demanded, Achaemenid administrative correspondence was conducted in <a href="/wiki/Old_Greek" title="Old Greek">Greek</a>, making it a widely used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bureaucratic" title="Bureaucratic">bureaucratic</a> language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranian.2C_E._Tucker_2001_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iranian.2C_E._Tucker_2001-4">[4]</a></sup> Even though the Achaemenids had extensive contacts with the Greeks and vice versa, and had conquered many of the Greek-speaking areas both in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> during different periods of the empire, the native Old Iranian sources provide no indication of Greek linguistic evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranian.2C_E._Tucker_2001_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Iranian.2C_E._Tucker_2001-4">[4]</a></sup> However, there is plenty of evidence (in addition to the accounts of Herodotus) that Greeks, apart from being deployed and employed in the core regions of the empire, also evidently lived and worked in the heartland of the Achaemenid Empire, namely Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranian.2C_E._Tucker_2001_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Iranian.2C_E._Tucker_2001-4">[4]</a></sup> For example, Greeks were part of the various ethnicities that constructed Darius' palace in <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>, apart from the Greek inscriptions found nearby there, and one short Persepolis tablet written in Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranian.2C_E._Tucker_2001_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Iranian.2C_E._Tucker_2001-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Customs">Customs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Customs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">

</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1688" data-file-width="2260" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg/220px-Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg/330px-Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg/440px-Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persia_-_Achaemenian_Vessels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An Achaemenid drinking vessel</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> mentions that the Persians were invited to great birthday feasts (Herodotus, <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a></i> 8), which would be followed by many desserts, a treat which they reproached the Greeks for omitting from their meals. He also observed that the Persians drank wine in large quantities and used it even for counsel, deliberating on important affairs when drunk, and deciding the next day, when sober, whether to act on the decision or set it aside. Bowing to superiors, or royalty was one of the many Persian customs adopted by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farvahar001.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="977" data-file-width="1300" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Farvahar001.JPG/220px-Farvahar001.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Farvahar001.JPG/330px-Farvahar001.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Farvahar001.JPG/440px-Farvahar001.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farvahar001.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bas-relief of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farvahar" title="Farvahar">Farvahar</a> at Persepolis, Iran</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amulet-pendant_Louvre_Sb3906.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="890" data-file-width="800" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Amulet-pendant_Louvre_Sb3906.jpg/220px-Amulet-pendant_Louvre_Sb3906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Amulet-pendant_Louvre_Sb3906.jpg/330px-Amulet-pendant_Louvre_Sb3906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Amulet-pendant_Louvre_Sb3906.jpg/440px-Amulet-pendant_Louvre_Sb3906.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amulet-pendant_Louvre_Sb3906.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The image of a lioness used as a pendant, late 6th–4th centuries BC, from Susa – Department of Oriental Antiquities, <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It was during the Achaemenid period that <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> reached South-Western Iran, where it came to be accepted by the rulers and through them became a defining element of Persian culture. The religion was not only accompanied by a formalization of the concepts and divinities of the traditional Iranian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> but also introduced several novel ideas, including that of <a href="/wiki/Free_will_in_theology" title="Free will in theology">free will</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under the patronage of the Achaemenid kings, and by the 5th century BC as the <i>de facto</i> religion of the state, Zoroastrianism reached all corners of the empire. The Bible claims that <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> allowed the Jews to return to their <a href="/wiki/Homeland" title="Homeland">homeland</a> after decades of captivity by the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylonian</a> empires.</p>
<p>During the reign of Artaxerxes I and Darius II, Herodotus wrote "[the Perses] have no images of the gods, no temples nor altars, and consider the use of them a sign of folly. This comes, I think, from their not believing the gods to have the same nature with men, as the Greeks imagine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus.2C_I.131_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herodotus.2C_I.131-149">[148]</a></sup> He claims the Persians offer sacrifice to: "the sun and moon, to the earth, to fire, to water, and to the winds. These are the only gods whose worship has come down to them from ancient times. At a later period they began the worship of <a href="/wiki/Urania" title="Urania">Urania</a>, which they borrowed from the Arabians and Assyrians. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mylitta" title="Mylitta">Mylitta</a> is the name by which the Assyrians know this <a href="/wiki/Goddess" title="Goddess">goddess</a>, to whom the Persians referred as <a href="/wiki/Anahita" title="Anahita">Anahita</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus.2C_I.131_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herodotus.2C_I.131-149">[148]</a></sup> (The original name here is <a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">Mithra</a>, which has since been explained to be a confusion of Anahita with Mithra, understandable since they were commonly worshipped together in one temple).</p>
<p>From the Babylonian scholar-priest <a href="/wiki/Berossus" title="Berossus">Berosus</a>, who—although writing over seventy years after the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II Mnemon</a>—records that the emperor had been the first to make <a href="/wiki/Cult" title="Cult">cult</a> statues of divinities and have them placed in temples in many of the major cities of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> Berosus also substantiates Herodotus when he says the Persians knew of no images of gods until Artaxerxes II erected those images. On the means of sacrifice, Herodotus adds "they raise no altar, light no fire, pour no libations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus.2C_I.132_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herodotus.2C_I.132-151">[150]</a></sup> This sentence has been interpreted to identify a critical (but later) accretion to Zoroastrianism. An altar with a wood-burning fire and the <a href="/wiki/Yasna" title="Yasna">Yasna</a> service at which libations are poured are all clearly identifiable with modern Zoroastrianism, but apparently, were practices that had not yet developed in the mid-5th century. Boyce also assigns that development to the reign of Artaxerxes II (4th century BC), as an orthodox response to the innovation of the shrine cults.</p>
<p>Herodotus also observed that "no prayer or offering can be made without a <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">magus</a> present"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus.2C_I.132_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herodotus.2C_I.132-151">[150]</a></sup> but this should not be confused with what is today understood by the term <i>magus</i>, that is a <i>magupat</i> (modern Persian: <i>mobed</i>), a Zoroastrian priest. Nor does Herodotus' description of the term as one of the tribes or castes of the Medes necessarily imply that these <i>magi</i> were Medians. They simply were a hereditary priesthood to be found all over Western Iran and although (originally) not associated with any one specific religion, they were traditionally responsible for all ritual and religious services. Although the unequivocal identification of the <i>magus</i> with Zoroastrianism came later (Sassanid era, 3rd–7th century AD), it is from Herodotus' <i>magus</i> of the mid-5th century that Zoroastrianism was subject to doctrinal modifications that are today considered to be revocations of the original teachings of the prophet. Also, many of the ritual practices described in the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta's</a> <i>Vendidad</i> (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exposure_of_the_dead_(Zoroastrianism)" title="Exposure of the dead (Zoroastrianism)">exposure of the dead</a>) were already practiced by the <i>magu</i> of Herodotus' time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">

</table>

<div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lion_Darius_Palace_Louvre_Sb3298.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="980" data-file-width="1262" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Lion_Darius_Palace_Louvre_Sb3298.jpg/220px-Lion_Darius_Palace_Louvre_Sb3298.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Lion_Darius_Palace_Louvre_Sb3298.jpg/330px-Lion_Darius_Palace_Louvre_Sb3298.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Lion_Darius_Palace_Louvre_Sb3298.jpg/440px-Lion_Darius_Palace_Louvre_Sb3298.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lion_Darius_Palace_Louvre_Sb3298.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lion on a decorative panel from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I_the_Great" title="Darius I the Great">Darius I the Great</a>'s palace, Louvre</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Achaemenid architecture</i> includes large cities, temples, palaces, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mausoleums" title="Mausoleums">mausoleums</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Cyrus" title="Tomb of Cyrus">tomb of Cyrus</a> the Great. The quintessential feature of Persian architecture was its eclectic nature with elements of Median, Assyrian, and Asiatic Greek all incorporated, yet maintaining a unique Persian identity seen in the finished products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Achaemenid art</i> includes <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">frieze</a> reliefs, Metalwork such as the <a href="/wiki/Oxus_Treasure" title="Oxus Treasure">Oxus Treasure</a>, decoration of palaces, glazed brick masonry, fine craftsmanship (masonry, carpentry, etc.), and gardening. Although the Persians took artists, with their styles and techniques, from all corners of their empire, they produced not simply a combination of styles, but a synthesis of a new unique Persian style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup> Cyrus the Great in fact had an extensive ancient Iranian heritage behind him; the rich Achaemenid gold work, which inscriptions suggest may have been a specialty of the Medes, was for instance in the tradition of the delicate metalwork found in <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> II times at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasanlu" title="Hasanlu">Hasanlu</a> and still earlier at <a href="/wiki/Marlik" title="Marlik">Marlik</a>.</p>
<p>One of the most remarkable examples of both Achaemenid architecture and art is the grand palace of <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, and its detailed workmanship, coupled with its grand scale. In describing the construction of his palace at <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>, Darius the Great records that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Yaka timber was brought from <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandara</a> and from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carmania_(satrapy)" title="Carmania (satrapy)">Carmania</a>. The gold was brought from <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> ... the precious stone lapis-lazuli and carnelian ... was brought from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>. The turquoise from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chorasmia" title="Chorasmia">Chorasmia</a>, the silver and ebony from <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the ornamentation from <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>, the ivory from <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Arachosia" title="Arachosia">Arachosia</a>. The stone-cutters who wrought the stone, those were Ionians and <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardians</a>. The goldsmiths were <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a>. The men who wrought the wood, those were Sardians and Egyptians. The men who wrought the baked brick, those were Babylonians. The men who adorned the wall, those were Medes and Egyptians.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>This was imperial art on a scale the world had not seen before. Materials and artists were drawn from all corners of the empire, and thus tastes, styles, and motifs became mixed together in an eclectic art and architecture that in itself mirrored the Persian empire.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1192" data-file-width="958" height="373" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg/300px-Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg/450px-Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg/600px-Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artaxerses_III_tomb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tomb of Artaxerxes II in <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tombs">Tombs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Tombs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many Achaemenid rulers built tombs for themselves. The most famous, <a href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Rustam" title="Naqsh-e Rustam">Naqsh-e Rustam</a>, is an ancient necropolis located about 12 km northwest of <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, with the tombs of four of the kings of the dynasty carved in this mountain: <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Darius_I" title="Tomb of Darius I">Darius I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_I" title="Artaxerxes I">Artaxerxes I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darius_II" title="Darius II">Darius II</a>. Other kings constructed their own tombs elsewhere. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a> preferred to carve their tombs beside their summer capital <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, the left tomb belonging to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> and the right tomb belonging to <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a>, the last Achaemenid king to have a tomb. The tomb of the founder of the Achaemenid dynasty, <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, was built in <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> (now a world heritage site).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Achaemenid Empire left a lasting impression on the heritage and the cultural identity of Asia, Europe, and the Middle East, as well as influencing the development and structure of future empires. In fact the Greeks and later on the Romans copied the best features of the Persian method of governing the empire, and vicariously adopted them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georg_W._F._Hegel" title="Georg W. F. Hegel">Georg W. F. Hegel</a> in his work <i><a href="/wiki/Lectures_on_the_Philosophy_of_History" title="Lectures on the Philosophy of History">The Philosophy of History</a></i> introduces the Persian Empire as the "first empire that passed away" and its people as the "first historical people" in history. According to his account;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>The Persian Empire is an empire in the modern sense – like that which existed in Germany, and the great imperial realm under the sway of Napoleon; for we find it consisting of a number of states, which are indeed dependant, but which have retained their own individuality, their manners, and laws. The general enactments, binding upon all, did not infringe upon their political and social idiosyncrasies, but even protected and maintained them; so that each of the nations that constitute the whole, had its own form of constitution. As light illuminates everything – imparting to each object a peculiar vitality – so the Persian Empire extends over a multitude of nations, and leaves to each one its particular character. Some have even kings of their own; each one its distinct language, arms, way of life and customs. All this diversity coexists harmoniously under the impartial dominion of Light ... a combination of peoples – leaving each of them free. Thereby, a stop is put to that <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarism</a> and ferocity with which the nations had been wont to carry on their destructive feuds.</dd>
</dl>
<p>The famous American orientalist, Professor <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Upham_Pope" title="Arthur Upham Pope">Arthur Upham Pope</a> (1881–1969) said:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> "The western world has a vast unpaid debt to the Persian Civilization!"</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Will_Durant" title="Will Durant">Will Durant</a>, the American historian and philosopher, during one of his speeches, "Persia in the History of Civilization", as an address before the <i><a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93America_Society" title="Iran–America Society">Iran–America Society</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a> on 21 April 1948, stated:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>For thousands of years Persians have been creating beauty. Sixteen centuries before Christ there went from these regions or near it ... You have been here a kind of watershed of civilization, pouring your blood and thought and art and religion eastward and westward into the world ... I need not rehearse for you again the achievements of your Achaemenid period. Then for the first time in known history an empire almost as extensive as the United States received an orderly government, a competence of administration, a web of swift communications, a security of movement by men and goods on majestic roads, equaled before our time only by the zenith of Imperial Rome.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_kings_and_rulers">Achaemenid kings and rulers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Achaemenid kings and rulers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unattested">Unattested</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Unattested">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There were 4 unattested kings who ruled as satraps to the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Median_Empire" title="Median Empire">Median Empire</a>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attested">Attested</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Attested">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There were 13 attested kings during the 220 years of the Achaemenid Empire's existence. The reign of <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II_of_Persia" title="Artaxerxes II of Persia">Artaxerxes II</a> was the longest, lasting 47 years</p>

</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_058.jpg"><img alt="Illustrerad Verldshistoria band I Ill 058.jpg" data-file-height="2433" data-file-width="1361" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_058.jpg/100px-Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_058.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_058.jpg/150px-Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_058.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_058.jpg/200px-Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_058.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>The founder of the empire, King of 4 corners of the world</td>
<td>560–530 BC</td>

<td>son of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_II_of_Persia" title="Darius II of Persia">Darius II</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>)</td>
</tr>

<p>Early in the reign of Artaxerxes II, in 399 BC, the Persians lost control over Egypt. They regained control 57 years later, in 342 BC, when <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a> conquered Egypt.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Achaemenid_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Daryaee, edited by Touraj; A. Shapour Shahbazi (2012). <a class="external text" href="http://www.oxfordhandbooks.com/view/10.1093/oxfordhb/9780199732159.001.0001/oxfordhb-9780199732159" rel="nofollow"><i>The Oxford handbook of Iranian history</i></a>. Oxford: Oxford University Press. p. 131. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-973215-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-973215-9">978-0-19-973215-9</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">29 December</span> 2016</span>. <q>Although the Persians and Medes shared domination and others were placed in important positions, the Achaemenids did not -- could not -- provide a name for their multinational state. Nevertheless, they referred to it as Khshassa, "the Empire".</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAchaemenid+Empire&amp;rft.au=A.+Shapour+Shahbazi&amp;rft.aufirst=edited+by+Touraj&amp;rft.aulast=Daryaee&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+handbook+of+Iranian+history&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.oxfordhandbooks.com%2Fview%2F10.1093%2Foxfordhb%2F9780199732159.001.0001%2Foxfordhb-9780199732159&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-973215-9&amp;rft.pages=131&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-EY-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-EY_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Ehsan_Yarshater" title="Ehsan Yarshater">Yarshater, Ehsan</a> (1993). <i>The Cambridge History of Iran, Volume 3</i>. <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">Cambridge University Press</a>. p. 482. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-20092-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-20092-9">978-0-521-20092-9</a>. <q>Of the four residences of the Achaemenids named by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> — <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> or <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> — the last [situated in Iraq] was maintained as their most important capital, the fixed winter quarters, the central office of bureaucracy, exchanged only in the heat of summer for some cool spot in the highlands. Under the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> the site of the Mesopotamian capital moved a little to the north on the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> — to <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>. It is indeed symbolic that these new foundations were built from the bricks of ancient <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, just as later <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, a little further upstream, was built out of the ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sassanian</a> double city of <a href="/wiki/Al-Mada%27in" title="Al-Mada'in">Seleucia-Ctesiphon</a>.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAchaemenid+Empire&amp;rft.aufirst=Ehsan&amp;rft.aulast=Yarshater&amp;rft.btitle=The+Cambridge+History+of+Iran%2C+Volume+3&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-20092-9&amp;rft.pages=482&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<table class="nowraplinks navbox-subgroup" style="border-spacing:0">
<tr>
<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:9.0em;font-weight:normal;font-size:85%;">3400–550 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_Common_Era" title="Before Common Era">BCE</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
